Richard Anthony Drago (June 25, 1945 - November 2, 2023) was an American relief pitcher in Major League Baseball who played for the Kansas City Royals (1969-1973), Boston Red Sox (1974-1975, 1978-1980), California Angels (1976-1977), Baltimore Orioles (1977), and Seattle Mariners (1981).

=== FULL EXTRACTED CONTENT ===


--- [Obituary: Dick Drago (1945-2023) - RIP Baseball] ---
RIP to Dick Drago, who won more than 100 games as a starter and reliever during a 13-year career. The Boston Globe reported that the 78-year-old died in Florida on November 2 of complications following surgery. Drago played for the Kansas City Royals (1969-73), Boston Red Sox (1974-75, 1978-80), California Angels (1976-77), Baltimore Orioles (1977) and Seattle Mariners (1981). Richard Anthony Drago was born in Toledo, OH, on June 25, 1946. He played baseball and basketball at Woodward High School in Toledo, and he also played plenty of Little League, Pony and American Legion ball during his summers. He was part of a Connie Mack team that reached the semi-finals of the national tournament in 1962. When he wasn’t pitching, Drago played all over the infield. He graduated from high school in 1963 and enrolled at the University of Detroit for a year. He signed as an amateur free agent with the Detroit Tigers in September of 1964, with scout Herm Kander doing the honors. After playing winter ball in 1963-64, Drago played for two different Class-A teams in ’64 and won 5 games between them while losing 14. He returned to Class-A Rocky Mount in 1966 and turned in a brilliant season. He won 15 games, including a 7-inning no-hitter in the first game of a doubleheader against Greensboro on May 15, 1966. Right after he accomplished the feat, his roommate, Darrell Clark, told him, “I guess I’ll have to go out and throw one, too.” And he did, in the second game. It was believed to be the first time in organized baseball that a team threw two no-hitters on the same day. Drago was just as happy for his roommate as he was for his own success. “I was in the stands holding my breath for Darrell to get his,” he said. “When it was over, I tried to be the first one out there, but some of the other guys beat me to him.” Drago won 15 games in 1967 and 1968, working his way up to Triple-A Toledo. His fastball was the pitch that got him signed by the Tigers, but part of his development involved learning a curveball and a slider. Johnny Sain, one of the great pitching instructors of all time, worked with the youngster in winter ball. “He can teach you how to pitch,” Drago said in a 1967 interview. “He had me throwing seven different pitches one time last year.” The success didn’t get Drago to the majors with Detroit, but it got him selected by the Kansas City Royals in the expansion draft held in October 1968. He made the team out of spring training to become an original Royal in 1969. Drago made his major-league debut in the Royals’ third game, throwing 4-2/3 shutout innings against Oakland on April 11. He spent the first month of the season as a long reliever before moving into the starting rotation in May. He threw back-to-back complete game wins over California and Baltimore in his first two starts, and even if the Royals didn’t always give him enough run support, he showed the ability to pitch well deep into ballgames. Drago’s success left Kansas City manager Joe Gordon wondering why Detroit never gave him a shot in the majors. “He’s as strong as a bull. He has a good slider, a sinker, an upshoot fastball, changes his speed well.” Pitching coach Mel Harder added that Drago began the season as a reliever because he didn’t show the big fastball in spring training that everyone claimed he had. “Then, about two weeks ago in Minnesota, he really popped the fastball in relief. Drago said everything seemed to jell all at once,” Harder added. Drago moved in and out of the rotation for the rest of the year, largely depending on how effective his last start was. He tossed a couple of shutouts in August, and that seemed to cement his role as a starter. The one thing he didn’t show was an ability to hit. In fact, Drago didn’t get his first major-league hit until September 4 against the Seattle Pilots. The timing was perfect, as he cracked an RBI double against Diego Segui that scored Buck Martinez from second base, adding an insurance run in a 5-3 win. Drago made a total of 26 starts in his 41 appearances with Kansas City, with an 11-13 record and a 3.77 ERA. He completed 10 games and was second on the team with 200-2/3 innings pitched. For the rest of his time with the Royals, Drago was one of Kansas City’s most reliable starters. He led the team in innings pitched from 1970-72, even though he had just one season with a win-loss record of over .500. The results say more about the inconsistencies of the expansion Royals than it does Drago’s pitching abilities. The team went through three managers in its first two years. Gordon was replaced by Charlie Metro in 1970, and he lasted through 52 games before being fired. Drago celebrated his departure and the appointment of Bob Lemon by throwing an 8-1 complete game over the Washington Senators. Metro, he said, “made it just miserable for us… It’s hard to explain how it was playing for him. There was no room for a mistake. Every pitch you made was supposed to produce a pop-up or a groundout. There was no room for any fun in the game. To Charlie, if you gave it all and lost, it wasn’t good enough.” Drago struggled to a 9-15 record in 1970, but he won 17 games for Kansas City and Lemon in 1971, as the team finished in a surprising second place in the AL West. He struck out just 109 batters in 241-1/3 innings. but he had pinpoint control, allowing 46 walks. He gave up more than a hit an inning, but his 2.98 ERA was his best mark as a starter, and he led Royals pitchers with 15 complete games and 4 shutouts. He finished fifth in the Cy Young Award vote. “I’ve always been a control pitcher,” Drago said. “But I’ve also learned, I think, to throw better to spots. You know we’ve got a good defensive team, so I get it over and they hit it at somebody. I give up a lot of hits, but a lot of them aren’t hits that hurt.” The Royals remained an above-.500 team, but Drago slumped after his 17-win year. He won 12 games in 1973 and ’74, but he lost 17 and 14 games, respectively. His ERA also climbed higher, to 3.01 in 1972 and 4.23 in 1973. He was supplanted as staff ace by Paul Splittorff, who became Kansas City’s first 20-game winner in 1973. The Royals traded Drago to the Boston Red Sox on October 24, 1973, getting starter Marty Pattin in return. With 61 wins, Drago was the Royals’ winningest pitcher at the time of the trade. “Drago has done a workman-like job, but you must always give someone up to get someone whom you feel will help your club’s chances,” said Royals GM Cedric Tallis. Boston was rebuilding its pitching staff in 1974, combining existing starters like Luis Tiant, Roger Moret and Bill Lee with newly acquired pitchers like Drago, Rick Wise, Juan Marichal and Reggie Cleveland. Drago started off in the bullpen, but injuries and ineffectiveness of some of the starters caused manager Darrell Johnson to put him in the rotation for long stretches. As a result, Drago became one of the busiest pitchers on the Sox. He pitched in 33 games and made 18 starts, with a 7-10 record and 3.48 ERA. In his return trip to Kansas City, he allowed a game-tying 2-run homer to Hal McRae, earning some heckling from his former teammates. Then he didn’t allow another run in 5-1/3 innings of relief, ultimately getting the win when Boston scored a run in the 13th inning to win 5-4. It would be the last season that Drago was used as a starter with any regularity. Going into 1975, Drago had a total of 4 career saves. He earned a save in his first appearance of the year by throwing a scoreless 13th inning against Baltimore in a 3-2 win. He wasn’t a full-time stopper, as Johnson gave plenty of save opportunities to Diego Segui and Jim Willoughby, too, but Drago ended up with 15 of Boston’s 31 saves. He had a 2-2 record, a 3.84 ERA and 43 strikeouts in 40 games. Boston needed those saves, as the Red Sox won 95 games to finish first in the AL East Division. Boston swept the Oakland A’s in the AL Championship Series, and Drago earned saves in 2 of the 3 wins. His World Series experience against Cincinnati was a mixed bag. He took the loss in Game Two after entering in relief for Lee. In the top of the ninth inning, with the game tied at 2 and Johnny Bench on second base after doubling against Lee, Drago allowed a single to Dave Concepcion and an RBI double to Ken Griffey to put the Reds ahead for good, 3-2. Then in Game Six, best remembered for Carlton Fisk’s dramatic home run, Drago pitched 3 scoreless innings in relief after Bernie Carbo had tied the game in the ninth inning with a 3-run blast. Rick Wise picked up the win by working a scoreless 12th inning, setting the stage for Fisk’s heroics. But Drago made it possible with a valuable, if little remembered, performance. Drago adapted to the relief role quickly. After giving up Griffey’s game-winning hit in Game Two, he told reporters after the game, “Sometimes you do, sometimes you don’t — that’s the life of a relief pitcher. I made good pitches. I did the job I wanted to do, only things didn’t work out for me.” The other part about the life of a relief pitcher is the frequent transactions, and Drago was no exception to that rule, either. On March 3, 1976, he was traded to the California Angels for outfielders John Balaz and Dick Sharon and shortstop Dave Machemer. The move dumbfounded his teammates, especially his fellow pitchers. “I’m very disappointed. The way Drago was treated last year wasn’t fair at all,” Lee said. The Angels were happy to have the veteran bring some balance to its bullpen, but he ended up with just 6 saves in 43 games. It was still enough to lead the Angels, who had 17 saves as a team — when Nolan Ryan and Frank Tanana combine for 44 complete games, there aren’t many save opportunities to be had. Drago also found himself making a bit of baseball history when he gave up a homer to Milwaukee’s Henry Aaron on July 20, 1976. It was the 755th and final home run of Aaron’s career. Drago started 1977 with the Angels and saved 2 games in 13 appearances. He was traded to the Baltimore Orioles in mid-June for pitcher Dyar Miller and earned 3 more saves, giving him a total of 5 in 49 appearances. He won 6 games for Baltimore and had a 3.63 ERA as an Oriole. After the season, he was declared a free agent. Free agency at the time worked differently than it does now. All free agents were put into a re-entry draft, and they were free to negotiate with any team that drafted them. Drago was selected by 6 teams and was able to negotiate a return to Boston with the Red Sox by signing a multi-year, $300,000 contract. “I wanted to go back to Boston because I always liked it there,” he said. “The California Angels didn’t appreciate my value as a relief pitcher.” Drago returned to the Red Sox bullpen and picked up where he had left off. He wasn’t the team’s official closer, though he did lead the team with 13 saves in 1979. He had 7 saves in 1978, but he was, above all, a long reliever that year. Of his 37 appearances, 24 of them lasted multiple innings. The Red Sox suffered a collapse in 1978; the team went from leading the AL East by 9 games in August to losing a 1-game playoff with the Yankees to finish in second. Drago, though, did what he was asked to do. He threw 19-2/3 innings in September and had a 1.93 ERA in that span. In 1979, when he picked up 13 of Boston’s 29 saves, only 3 of them were an inning or less. He pitched 89 innings that year while appearing in 53 games, and he also picked up 10 wins while logging a 3.03 ERA for the second straight year. Drago had a much more uneven season in 1980. He gave up a grand slam to Milwaukee’s Sixto Lezcano in his first appearance of the season, and he had frequent rough outings that sent his ERA at or above 5. He was moved into the starting rotation in September and pitched much better. Drago won 3 of his 7 starts and had a 3.27 ERA as a starter. One of his better relief outings came on July 13 against Detroit. He came into the sixth inning in relief of Bob Ojeda and threw 3-1/3 innings of hitless relief. The only blemish was a walk to Steve Kemp. Boston scored 4 runs in those innings, giving the Red Sox the 8-4 win and Drago the 100th win of his career. Boston traded Drago to the Seattle Mariners on April 8, 1981, in exchange for reliever Manny Sarmiento. Drago, bluntly, said that the trade “stinks.” “I would have been better off if [Boston] had released me so that I could have made my own deal. I guess they don’t think too much of my loyalty.” Drago pitched in 39 games for the Mariners and picked up 5 saves and a 4-6 record, but it took a 6-inning scoreless relief outing in his final appearance to drop his ERA below 6 to a 5.53 mark. Nearly a year to the day after the trade, the Mariners released him, bringing his MLB career to a close. Drago spent 1982 pitching for the Mexico City Tigers in the Mexican League before ending his professional pitching career. In 13 years in the majors, Drago had a 108-117 record. He started 189 of his 519 games and threw 62 complete games, including 10 shutouts. He also earned 58 saves. Drago had a 3.62 ERA, a 103 ERA+, and a 1.311 WHIP. He struck out 987 batters and walked 558. As a batter, he had 21 hits in 174 at-bats for a .077/.145/.091 slash line. Drago was an avid golfer and bowler and did plenty of both in his retirement as a player. He worked for a vice president of sales for a Massachusetts promotional services company after he left the game. He later moved to Fort Myers, FL, and worked in sales and marketing for several companies. He also pitched in the Senior League in Florida. Drago’s role in the Red Sox bullpen during the team’s memorable 1970s teams left him a permanent part of Boston’s sports memory. One of his most memorable games was one in which he didn’t even play. It was that 1978 playoff game between the Yankees and Red Sox to determine the AL East division winner. “That was the hardest defeat in all the years I played, losing that playoff game after we both won 99 games,” Drago said in 1989. “For three days I sat in the chair in the living room. My wife dusted around me.” Follow me on Instagram: @rip_mlb Follow me on Facebook: ripbaseball Follow me on Bluesky: @ripmlb Follow me on Threads: @rip_mlb Support RIP Baseball Discover more from RIP Baseball Subscribe to get the latest posts sent to your email.


--- [Dick Drago - Wikipedia] ---
Richard Anthony Drago (June 25, 1945 – November 2, 2023) was an American relief pitcher in Major League Baseball who played for the Kansas City Royals (1969–1973), Boston Red Sox (1974–1975, 1978–1980), California Angels (1976–1977), Baltimore Orioles (1977), and Seattle Mariners (1981). He batted and threw right-handed. Drago is notable for being the final pitcher to give up a home run to Hank Aaron.
